Basic Stratagem for the Wife of a Missing Husband
Presents the basic hila and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi stratagem for the wife of a missing husband and identify the conditions under which she may remarry after the waiting period.

Core Idea
The wife of a missing husband waits four years then observes the iddah of death – no harm and no causing harm.

This lesson unpacks the primary ruling for a wife whose husband is lost and communication is impossible. The stratagem (hila) is rooted in the practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab and the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar'. We will parse the conditions, the waiting period, and the requirement (or lack thereof) for a judge's permission – a point of difference with the Maliki school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the statement attributed to 'Umar: the wife waits four years, then observes iddah for four months and ten days, then becomes permissible for remarriage.
This is the foundation of the Hanafi position.
2
Examine the proof
The text cites a narration from Malik via Yahya ibn Sa'id from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib from 'Umar, and a similar narration from Ibn Wahb. Also the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no causing harm) is cited as a general principle.
The proof is from practice of a Companion (athan) and a general hadith.
3
Analyze the second issue: need for ruler's permission
The text asks whether the waiting period requires a ruler's order. The answer 'known from what preceded' implies no necessity, as 'Umar did not mention it. Then it mentions Malik's view and Ibn Farhun's support for requiring a judge.
This is a thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence): Hanafi says no judge needed; Maliki says yes.
4
Apply to colonized lands
The third and fourth issues address lands like Egypt, Syria, India where non-Muslim rulers exist. The ruling: if Muslim judges are present and investigation possible, the wife is treated as in Islamic lands – same waiting period.
This applies the same principle, distinguishing from pure dar al-harb.

Source Text

العبد الفقير محمد الشهير بالفاها شمر بن احمد له وال مع الاخوان فى عناية الحمد المحاق - طريق تطليق زوجة المفقود الذي تعذر الارسال اليه فوارسل اليه فتعاند عن خبره ثم اعتدت كالوفاة وسقطت بها النفقة، The poor slave Muhammad, famously known as Ifa' Shamar bin Ahmad, with his brothers in the care of praise of the Hamid - the way of divorcing the wife of the missing husband whose communication became difficult, so he sent to him [the husband] but he became obstinate about his news, then she observed iddah like death and maintenance fell away. The proof of that is what Malik narrated from Yahya bin Sa'id from Sa'id bin al-Musayyib from 'Umar bin al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'Any woman whose husband disappears and she does not know where he is, she waits four years, then observes iddah for four months and ten days, then becomes permissible.' And what Ibn Wahb narrated from 'Abd al-Jabbar from Ibn Shihab that 'Umar bin al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) set for the missing husband from the day of his departure four years, then ordered her to observe the iddah of a woman whose husband has died, then she may do with herself as she wishes when her iddah ends. And in the hadith: 'No harm and no causing harm.' As for the second issue, its answer is known from what preceded it, and it is the statement of Malik. It is said: 'Should she observe the four-year period without the ruler's order?' And Qadi Ibn Farhun supported [the need for ruler's order] in his book Tabsirat al-Hukkam fi Usul al-Aqdiyyah wa Manahij al-Ahkam in the chapter on what requires the ruling of a judge, that the suspension concerning absentees and others does not [depend] on a judge's ruling? As for the third issue, its answer, and Allah knows best, is that the Muslim woman whose husband is lost in lands that the disbelievers have overtaken for a long period, as in Egypt, Syria, and India, she waits four years then observes the iddah of death for four months and ten days, and her husband is counted among the first category of the missing ones because they define it as he whose news is cut off and it is possible to investigate him. And the second category is defined as the one missing in the land of war: he who is absent, his news cut off, and it is not possible to investigate him because he is lost in the land of war. As for the mentioned lands, even if the ruler is a disbeliever, they are not like the land of war in every respect due to the presence of judges there and their narrators and the possibility of investigating him. So I advise that her ruling is the ruling of one who lost her husband in the land of Islam, so she does not wait until [the end?] of life. As for the fourth issue, its answer is also known from what preceded it: that there is a difference between the missing in the land of Islam and the missing in the colonized lands because of what we mentioned of the presence of Muslim judges there and the possibility of investigation. On this basis, the second scenario mentioned in the Mukhtasar is not specific to the Muslim woman in the land of Islam; it includes whoever is in the lands colonized by disbelievers, because we mentioned that what is meant by 'mushrik lands' are the Arab lands that a Muslim cannot reach, so the judges cannot investigate there, not all disbelieving lands, because they may be peaceful or... And as for the one residing in the mushrik war lands, we rule her and her husband with the ruling of Muslims; the Imam ransoms them from the treasury if there is one, otherwise from his own wealth, and if not, then it is obligatory on all Muslims. (Then a sentence in Urdu: 'Due to lack of arrangement of maintenance, demand for separation from the missing to...') As for the fifth issue, its answer is that the woman whose husband is missing and he left her no maintenance, and she is in extreme need or fears corruption for herself, she has the right to divorce without delay, as is the meaning of the condition in the saying of Shaykh Khalil.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This is a hila case (legal stratagem) providing a way for a wife of a missing husband to obtain a divorce. The passage begins with a title identifying the stratagem: 'The way of divorcing the wife of the missing husband.' It then presents the ruling attributed to 'Umar ibn al-Khattab: the wife waits four years, then observes the iddah of a widow (four months and ten days), after which she may remarry. This is supported by a hadith: 'No harm and no causing harm.' The first issue establishes the basic ruling. The second issue asks whether this waiting period requires a ruler's permission; the answer is known from the preceding (the basic ruling does not require permission), though the text mentions Malik's school and Ibn Farhun's view that the ruler's judgment is needed. The third issue addresses a wife whose husband is lost in lands overtaken by non-Muslims (e.g., Egypt, Syria, India). Despite the ruler being non-Muslim, because there are Muslim judges and possibility of investigation, she is treated as if missing in Islamic lands: she waits four years then observes iddah of death. The fourth issue distinguishes between Islamic lands and colonized lands: colonized lands with Muslim judges are like Islamic lands, so the rule applies. The fifth issue states that if the wife has no maintenance and is in dire need or fears immorality, she may obtain a divorce immediately without waiting. The passage concludes with an Urdu sentence about demanding separation due to lack of maintenance, and clarifies that the wife's right to divorce is unconditional in such circumstances.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A husband who is absent with no known location and whose news is cut off.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death or disappearance of her husband before remarrying.
تطليق (tatliq): The divorce granted to the wife, in this case by a judge or through the hila.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): A hadith meaning 'No harm and no causing harm' – a principle used to permit the stratagem.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on whether the wife needs a ruler's permission to observe the waiting period?
A) She must obtain permission from a Muslim ruler.
B) She does not need permission; the waiting period is self-administered.
C) She needs permission only if the husband is missing in non-Muslim lands.
D) The issue is not addressed in the passage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi stratagem for a missing husband's wife: wait four years, then observe iddah of death, without needing a judge's permission.

Fatwa on Faskh of Marriage for Missing and Defective Husbands
Fatwa request from Maliki scholars on faskh of marriage for missing and defective husbands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five scenarios requiring dissolution of marriage (faskh) in the Maliki school as presented in this istifta, and apply the Maliki approach to evaluate reversibility.

Core Idea
A missing husband does not leave the wife in perpetual limbo — Maliki fiqh provides faskh avenues with nuanced rules on reversibility.

This passage is a fatwa request (istifta) from Maliki scholars to a Maliki teacher in Medina, posing five cases of marital hardship: missing husband, insolvent, harsh regarding dower, impotent, and insane. The core question is whether dissolution reverts if the husband returns or improves. As a hila (legal stratagem) case, this fatwa seeks to operationalize Maliki procedures to free the wife from a harmful marriage. Responding to such questions requires knowledge of Maliki family law and its interplay with Hanafi jurisprudence, as this istifta appears in a Hanafi work on hilal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose and parties
Recognize that this is a fatwa request (istifta) from Maliki scholars to a Maliki jurist. The goal is to obtain rulings on practical cases where a wife is trapped in a marriage due to husband's absence or defect.
The responder is al-Allama al-Salih al-Tunisi, a Maliki teacher in the Prophet's Mosque.
2
List the five scenarios
Extract the five distinct cases: (1) missing husband returns after faskh, (2) insolvent husband becomes wealthy after faskh, (3) harsh husband becomes easy after faskh, (4) impotent husband, (5) insane husband. Each case requires a ruling on whether the dissolution stands or the marriage reverts.
The text uses keywords: al-mafqud, al-mu'sir, al-muta'annit, al-'annin, al-majnun.
3
Analyze the legal stratagem (hila) aspect
The fatwa request is a hila because it seeks a mechanism to lawfully dissolve the marriage. The questions imply that in some Maliki positions, dissolution may be conditional or reversible. Understanding the hila requires knowing the conditions under which the qadi can grant faskh.
The presence of the questions in a Hanafi work indicates cross-madhhab use of Maliki rulings as a hila.
4
Examine the role of the qadi
Several questions ask whether a substitute acts for the husband or whether the qadi's judgment is needed. In Maliki fiqh, faskh often requires a qadi's decree, especially for impotence and insanity.
The text: 'هل يفرق عن زوجها ام لا وهل يقوم مقامه ام يحتاج الى قضا القاضي'
5
Note the reversibility issue
The first three scenarios ask if the dissolution reverts when the husband's condition improves. This reveals that Maliki law may treat faskh as revocable in certain circumstances, unlike the general rule that faskh is irrevocable.
The question 'فهل ترد اليهم ازواجهن' indicates possible return of wives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستفتاء من العلماء المالكية ثانيا (1) ما قولكم رجہ کو اللہ تعالیٰ فیما اذا رجع المفقود او ايسر المعسراو (۱) اطماع المتعنت بعد فسخ نكاحه فهل ترد اليهم از واحهم املا (ب) مالراد من العارف والمهمات في قول المالكية ان الواحدانا كان مدل ارنا يرجع اليه في المهمات يكفر عن جماعة المسلمين التي يحتاج إليها عند عدم الحاكم حسار و اعتبارا. Second: The Istifta' from the Maliki scholars: (1) What do you say, may Allah bless you, regarding if the missing husband returns, or the insolvent becomes wealthy, or the one who is harsh in her dower becomes easy after the dissolution of marriage—do they return to their wives or not? (b) What is meant by 'al-arif' and 'al-muhimmat' in the Maliki saying that the two wives? ... (3) What is the ruling of the wife of the impotent man with you—is she separated from her husband or not, and does someone else take his place or does it require the qadi's judgment or who? (m) Similarly, the insane—is his wife divorced if she is such? And if the first, what is the way to it? The answer from al-Allama al-Salih al-Tunisi, teacher in the Prophet's Mosque in Medina: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, the Wise, the All-Knowing... After, the following questions came from a brother... requesting clarification... to reach a ruling on the issues mentioned according to the Maliki school. Here is the summary of the questions: (Q) If the marriage is dissolved...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a fatwa request from Maliki scholars. It poses several questions regarding faskh (dissolution) of marriage: (1) If a missing husband returns, or an insolvent husband becomes able to pay maintenance, or a husband who was harsh about dower becomes easy, does the dissolution revert and the wife return? (2) Clarification of a term in Maliki fiqh about the role of 'arif' and 'muhimmat'. (3) The wife of an impotent man (anin) — is she separated, and does a substitute act for the husband? (4) The insane — can his wife be divorced? The answer begins with the basmala and a preamble, indicating the responder will address these issues according to the Maliki school. This fits into the broader work on hilal (legal stratagems), as it seeks mechanisms to dissolve marriages in difficult situations.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqūd): A husband who is missing and whose whereabouts are unknown.
العنين (al-'annīn): A husband who is impotent and cannot consummate marriage.
المعسر (al-mu'sir): A husband who is insolvent and cannot pay maintenance.
المتعنت (al-muta'annit): A husband who is harsh or difficult, especially regarding dower.
المجنون (al-majnūn): A husband who is insane.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Dissolution of a marriage contract by a qadi or by mutual consent due to a defect or hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Missing husband returning after faskh
B) Insolvent husband becoming wealthy after faskh
C) Husband guilty of domestic violence
D) Impotent husband

Key Takeaway
The fatwa request highlights that Maliki fiqh provides conditional dissolution of marriage in cases of hardship, with the possibility of revival if the husband's condition improves, unlike the Hanafi view which tends toward finality.

When a Second Marriage Does Not Nullify the First: The Two Exceptions
Explains two exceptions to the rule that consummation by a second husband nullifies the first marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two exceptional cases where a wife returns to her first husband even after consummation by a second husband, and apply the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy to determine the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In two cases, the wife returns to the first husband even after the second consummates: testified death or maintenance annulment.

This passage from Khalil's Mukhtasar and al-Zarqani's commentary clarifies a critical hila for women whose husbands are missing or fail to provide maintenance. While the general rule is that consummation by a second husband establishes the second marriage, two exceptions allow the wife to return to the first husband. Understanding these exceptions and their conditions is essential for issuing correct fatwas in family law cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The base ruling from Khalil: divorce is realized by the entry of the second husband (i.e., consummation nullifies the first marriage).
This is the default: if the first husband returns after the second consummates, she does not return.
2
Note the two exceptions
The text specifies two cases where entry of the second does not forfeit the wife: (a) absent husband testified dead who returns, (b) wife divorced for lack of maintenance that is later proven to have been available.
These are the only two exceptions according to this hila.
3
Understand the rationale
In these cases, the basis for the second marriage was flawed (false testimony or erroneous annulment), so the first marriage revives.
The hila protects the wife from permanence of a marriage based on mistake.
4
Check the conditions for the hila
The annulment (tafriq) must be by a judge or by a group of three Muslims if no judge exists. One person alone is not sufficient.
This is a procedural condition that affects the validity of the annulment.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that the two exceptions are valid and the wife returns; this is the well-known (mashhur) view. The requirement of three witnesses for the annulment is also the stronger view.
Later fatwa practice follows this, as per the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المستفاد من قول خليل وقدر طلاق يتحقق بدخول الثاني وتفريح الزرقاني عليه يقوله فان جاء الاول قبل دخول الثاني كان حق بها - واذا كان الغائب مشهود عليه بالموت فقدم او ثبت حياته او طلقت زوجته لانقطاع النفقة فتبين استمرارها ففي هاتين الصورتين لا تفوت بدخول الثاني بل ترجع للدول ولو ولدت الاولاد وهو مفاد خليل في المنحى لها زوجها مع حل الزرقاني عليه مصورالها بقوله من شهدت بينه شهدت فتزوجت ثم قدم فلا تفوت بدخوله كما يفيده قوله یعنی خليلا في الاستحقاق مشهود بموته و في القضاء والقص ان ثبت كدبهم كحياة من قتل وقوله والمطلقة لعدم النفقة ثم ظهر اسقاطها المصطوفة والمعطوط عليها المسائل المشتركة في الحكم المخبريه عليها بقوله فلا تفوت بدخول ونصور الزرقاني لها بقوله بان اقام بيته انه كان يرسلها اليها اوانها وصلتها اوانه تركها عندها فلا يفتيها دخول الثاني و تاييد البناني ذلك تنقل فلاته المواق ونصه و اما مسئلة المطلقة لعدم النفقة فقال الـ عبد الرحمن ان ثبت بعد قدومه لريتها لها ردت له ولو دخل بها متزوجها كما ذكر. عدم نفقہ کی وجہ سے فسخ نکاح کا مطالبہ عن ابن عرفة عن ابن يونس و مثله اقرب المسالك يقوله بخلاف المتعى لها زوجها والمطلقة لعدم النفقه ثم ظهر سقوطها يعنى فله نفوت بدخول الثاني كما صرح به في اخو النظائر ومثله المجموع بقوله وان نهى لها تبين الكذب اوطلق عليه لعدم النفقة ترتبين إسقاطها لم تفت بدخول - ج عن س) الذي عليه الجمه وروبة العمل وهوا المشهوران فاك التفريق رومسائله والمتعلق به للحاكم فان عدم حسا او اعتباط فجماعة المسلمين الثلاثة فما فوق علوم مقامه ولا يلغى الواحد في مثل هذا والما نسب ذلك للاجهورى فى احدى الروايتين عنه وتبعه بعض السراج من العربين والاول هو الذى عليه المعول وعليه فلا لزوم معرفة هذا الواحد ولا لبيان المهمات الذي يرجع فيها اليه على ان ذلك واصح وهي كناية عن كونه عالما عا فلا مرجعا الاهل جمهته في حل مشكلاتهم مطلقات
Derived from the statement of Khalil: 'And the extent of divorce is realized by the entry of the second [husband]' and al-Zarqani's commentary on it says: 'If the first comes before the entry of the second, she is his right.' And if the absent husband is attested to have died, then he arrives or his life is proven, or his wife is divorced due to lack of maintenance and then it becomes clear that it continued, then in these two cases she does not forfeit [her right] by the entry of the second, but rather returns to the first, even if she gave birth to children...
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage discusses a hila (legal stratagem) for a wife whose husband is missing or presumed dead, or who obtained a divorce due to lack of maintenance. The ruling: if the first husband returns before the second husband consummates the marriage, she returns to the first. However, in two specific cases—when the husband was witnessed dead and then returns alive, or when she divorced due to lack of maintenance and then it is proven that maintenance was available—she returns to the first even if the second husband had consummated the marriage. The text also notes that the annulment (tafriq) requires a judge or, in his absence, a group of three Muslims. This is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
بدخول الثاني (bi dukhūl al-thānī): By the entry (consummation) of the second husband
غائب مشهود عليه بالموت (ghā'ib mashhūd 'alayhi bi al-mawt): An absent husband for whom witnesses testify to his death
مطلقة لعدم النفقة (muṭallaqah li 'adam al-nafaqah): A wife divorced due to the husband's failure to provide maintenance

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) If the first husband returns after the second consummates the marriage and the case is not one of the two exceptions
C) If the first husband returns after she gives birth to a child from the second
D) If the first husband was only missing without testimony of death

Key Takeaway
In two specific cases—testified death and maintenance annulment—the wife returns to her first husband even if the second consummated the marriage.

Judicial Annulment for Impotent and Insane Husbands
Ruling on annulment for impotent and insane husbands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the waiting period for an impotent husband and apply it to similar defects such as insanity and leprosy.

Core Idea
The impotent husband gets one year from the judge's ruling; thereafter the wife may seek annulment.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on judicial annulment (tafriq) when the husband is affected by impotence or other defects that prevent consummation. The key is the one-year waiting period, which begins not from marriage but from the date of the judge's judgment. This period gives the husband an opportunity to recover, after which the wife has the right to annulment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Defect and Its Legal Effect
Impotence ('unan) is a defect preventing intercourse, entitling the wife to seek annulment. The text also covers insanity, leprosy, and vitiligo.
2
State the Waiting Period
The text prescribes a postponement of one year for the impotent husband.
3
Determine the Starting Point
The year is counted from the day of the judge's judgment, not from the wife's complaint or marriage.
Emphasized in the Arabic: 'من يوم الحكم'
4
Identify Additional Defects Covered
The same ruling applies to insanity, leprosy, and vitiligo, as stated: 'وحكم زوجه المجنون مثل حكم المعترض هو العنين'.
5
Note the Conditions for Annulment
The wife must bring the matter to a judge (or in absence, a group of Muslims). If the husband recovers during the year, annulment is not granted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زوجہ عنین کی تفریق اور عین کو مہلت کب سے شمار ہوگی؟
رج عن من) وهوان الحكم في زوجة الفتين التاجيل سنة من يوم الحكم والتراضى من طرف الحاكم وجماعة المسلمين كما هو والتفريق كذلك على ان السنين يطلق باطلا فين على مسترخي الذكر و عليه فالحكم ما تفررو على بغيره كانو و الذى لابتانى معه الجماع وهذا الزوجته الخيار في الحال ولا محتاج الى ضرب احال رج عن سل هو ان حكم زوجه المجنون مثل حكم المعترض هو العنين على التقسير الأول من التاجيل سنة والتقرني على حسب امروان ذلك بشير صاحب التحفة وبقوله . وحيث عيب الزوج باعتراض او برص وقيم عند القاضى اجلد الي تمام عام كذلك في المجنون والجدام وحده يحكم بالطاقة ان علمو الير على الاطلاق
Annulment of the impotent husband's wife and when is the waiting period counted for the impotent? - (From) and the ruling concerning the wife of the impotent is postponement for a year from the day of the judgment, and satisfaction on the part of the judge and the group of Muslims, and the annulment is likewise... The ruling of the wife of the insane is like the ruling of the impotent according to the first interpretation: postponement for a year and the decision according to his matter... And when the husband has a defect of leprosy or vitiligo and is brought before the judge, he is given a complete year. Similarly for the insane and the leprous, he is judged by the ability [to have intercourse] if they know absolutely.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for annulment (tafriq) in cases of the husband's impotence ('unan) or insanity. The Hanafi position: the impotent husband is given a one-year waiting period, starting from the date of the judge's ruling. If he cannot perform intercourse within that year, the wife has the option to seek annulment. The judge's involvement is required, or in his absence, a group of Muslims. The same ruling applies to the insane husband according to one narration. Additionally, defects like leprosy or vitiligo also entitle the wife to annulment after a year if intercourse is impossible.

Key Terms
عنين ('anīn): An impotent man, unable to have intercourse
تاجيل سنة (ta'jīl sanah): Postponement for one year given to the impotent husband from the date of the judge's judgment
تفريق (tafrīq): Judicial annulment of marriage due to a defect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From the date of marriage
B) From the date of the wife's complaint
C) From the date of the judge's judgment
D) From the date of consummation attempt

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants a one-year waiting period from the judge's ruling for impotent, insane, and similarly defective husbands; after that, the wife may obtain a judicial annulment.

Maliki Rulings on Missing Husband and Ignorant Divorcée
Fatwa citing Muwatta and Mudawwana on two related cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Maliki ruling for a missing husband's wife and evaluate when a Hanafi mufti may adopt this non-Hanafi position.

Core Idea
The Maliki school provides a time-bound hila for women in suspended marriages, differing from the Hanafi approach.

This fatwa from the Maliki scholar Saeed bin Siddiq al-Fulani cites the Muwatta and Mudawwana to clarify two critical cases: a woman whose husband is missing (mafqud) and a woman who is divorced but ignorant of her husband's revocation (raj'a). Understanding these rulings is essential for a Hanafi mufti when considering non-Hanafi positions for necessity (darura) or general need (haja).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Distinguish the case of the missing husband (mafqud) from the divorced woman unaware of raj'a. Note their common feature: the woman's ignorance of her husband's status.
2
Trace the Maliki narrative
Follow the quotes from the Muwatta (Umar's statement) and the Mudawwana (Malik's earlier and later views) to see how the ruling evolved.
3
Apply the key distinction: consummation
Observe that Maliki's final position hinges on whether the second marriage was consummated. If not, the first husband has priority; if yes, she remains with the second.
4
Relate to Hanafi iftaa
Consider when a Hanafi mufti may adopt this Maliki hila, e.g., when the Hanafi requirement for a qadi's ruling is unavailable or excessively burdensome.

The answer from the scholar Saeed bin Siddiq al-Fulani, may Allah benefit us with his knowledge: In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. There is no power or strength except with Allah the Exalted. Glory be to You, we have no knowledge except what You have taught us, indeed You are the All-Knowing, the All-Wise. As for the prayer and peace upon our master Muhammad, the guide, the forbearing, and upon his family and companions, and those who come to his Lord with a sound heart. As for the answer about the wife of the missing husband in the Muwatta of the Imam of the Abode of Hijra and the Star of the Sunnah, Malik ibn Anas, may the mercy of the Lord of mankind and Paradise be upon him: Chapter on the waiting period of the woman who loses her husband. Yahya related to me from Malik from Yahya ibn Sa'id from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab that Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'Any woman who loses her husband and does not know where he is, she waits four years, then observes a waiting period of four months and ten days, then she becomes permissible (to remarry).' Malik said: 'If she marries after the completion of her waiting period, and her second husband consummates the marriage or does not consummate it, the first husband has no path to her.' He said: 'That is the established practice with us. And if her husband finds her before she marries, he has more right to her.' Malik said: 'I found people denying what some people said that Umar ibn al-Khattab said: that he gives the first husband a choice when he comes, either his dower or his wife.' Malik said: 'It has reached me that Umar ibn al-Khattab said regarding a woman whose husband divorces her while he is absent from her, and he files a revocation (raj'a) but it does not reach her, and he does [not] know that she has been informed, and her iddah expires and she marries: that if her second husband consummates the marriage, then she is forbidden (to the first), and there is no path for the first husband who divorced her.' Malik said: 'This is the best of what I have heard concerning this.' 231. 'This regarding the missing husband in the Mudawwana in the chapter of the missing husband.' I said: 'Do you see the woman whose husband is reported dead, so she observes iddah and then marries, and the woman whose husband divorces her while she is absent, so she learns of the divorce but does not know of the raj'a during the iddah, and her husband passes away? And the woman of the missing husband observes iddah of four years by order of the ruler, then four months and ten days, then she marries. Do these three cases have the same ruling according to Malik?' He said: 'No. As for the woman whose husband is reported dead, she is separated from her second husband and returned to her first husband after an istibra' of three menstrual cycles, even if she has children from him. As for the woman of the missing husband who is divorced and does not know of the raj'a – Malik used to say at one time: if she marries and her second husband has not consummated the marriage, then the first husbands have no path to them. Then Malik shortly before his death by about a year said regarding the divorced woman when her first husband returns and the second husband has not consummated: Malik said: her first husband has a right to her.' He said: 'I heard from him regarding the missing husband that he said: he has more right to her as long as the second husband has not consummated with her. And I see in both cases that her husbands, if they find them before the second husbands consummate, then the first husbands have more right, and if they consummate, then the later ones (i.e., the second husbands) [have more right].' And Ashhab said similar to his statement and chose what he chose. And al-Mughira and others said: 'Malik [said] the first.' And they said: 'A woman with two husbands, they inherit from their husbands and return to other than him.' He said: 'And do not consider it permissible to take the private part after the ruler's permission like a marriage contract, and her husband has come while he has not divorced nor died.' I said: 'Do you think that if her husband returns after four years and after four months and ten days, she is returned to him according to Malik's statement and he has more right to her?' He said: 'Yes.' I said: 'Then would she be with him on two divorces?' He said: 'No, rather she is with him on three divorces according to Malik, and that is how it is.'
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage opens with a brief concluding remark on a previous issue (irrevocable divorce with no recourse) and then presents a detailed fatwa from the Maliki scholar Saeed bin Siddiq al-Fulani. The fatwa cites the Muwatta of Imam Malik and the Mudawwana to clarify rulings for two critical cases: (1) a woman whose husband is missing (mawqud), and (2) a woman who is divorced but does not learn of her husband's revocation of divorce (raj'a) within the iddah and consequently remarries. For the missing husband, the Maliki school holds that the wife must wait four years, then observe an iddah of four months and ten days, after which she may remarry. If the original husband returns before her second marriage, he has the superior claim. If she has already married and consummation occurred, the first husband loses all rights. For the divorced woman, if she remarries due to ignorance of the raj'a, and the second marriage is not consummated, the first husband is more entitled; if consummated, the second marriage stands. The passage notes internal Maliki disagreements (e.g., whether the first husband has a choice) but concludes with the settled position of Malik shortly before his death: the first husband's right is extinguished only after consummation. This fatwa illustrates how the Maliki school provides a legal mechanism (hila) for women to regain freedom from a stalled marriage, which is relevant in Hanafi iftaa when considering non-Hanafi positions for necessities.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A husband whose whereabouts are completely unknown, leading to a special waiting period.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.
الرجعة (al-raj'a): The husband's right to take back his wife during the iddah of a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Malik's final position as cited in the Mudawwana, when does the first husband lose all rights to his wife after she remarries?
A) Immediately after she marries the second husband
B) Only if the second husband consummates the marriage
C) After four years of marriage
D) Never; he always retains a right to her

Key Takeaway
The Maliki school provides a time-bound hila for women of missing husbands, and a Hanafi mufti may adopt it under necessity, but must carefully apply the condition of consummation.

Maliki Ruling on the Missing Husband: The Three Scenarios
Maliki juridical dialogue on mafqud scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the Maliki ruling for three scenarios involving a missing husband, and distinguish the Hanafi position on each.

Core Idea
In the Maliki school, the first husband's right depends on consummation of the second marriage.

This passage from Al-Mudawwana presents a comparative analysis of three marital scenarios involving a missing husband. The Maliki school distinguishes between cases where the second marriage is consummated and where it is not, with significant consequences for the wife's status. Understanding this ruling is crucial for a Hanafi mufti when encountering cross-madhhab questions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three cases: (a) husband reported dead, (b) husband divorces and takes back then disappears, (c) missing husband with sultan's order. Note the differences.
2
Extract the Maliki ruling for each
For (a) she is returned to first after istibra'. For (b) earlier view vs later view: later view gives first husband priority if no consummation. For (c) first is more entitled only if second has not consummated.
Pay attention to Malik's evolving opinion.
3
Identify the key distinction: consummation
The turning point is whether the second husband consummated the marriage. If consummated, the second marriage stands; if not, the first husband is entitled to take her back.
This is a major difference from the Hanafi school.
4
Contrast with the Hanafi position
In the Hanafi school, the wife of a missing husband cannot remarry without certainty of death or divorce. Her remarriage without confirmation would be invalid. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is much stricter and does not accept the Maliki distinction based on consummation.
A Hanafi mufti must be aware of this khiyāf (difference) when issuing fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا في المفقود في المدونة في باب المفقود. قلت: أرأيت المرأة ينعى لها زوجها فتعتد منه ثم تتزوج، والمرأة يطلقها زوجها مما فتعلم بالطلاق ويراجعها في العدة وقد خاب زوجها ولم تعلم بالرجعة حتى تنقضي العدة فتزوج، وامرأة المفقود تعتد أربع سنين بأمر السلطان ثم أربعة أشهر وعشرا فتنكح. أهمؤلاء عند مالك تعملهن عمل واحد؟ قال: لا. أما التي ينعى لها زوجها فهذه يفرق بينها وبين زوجها الثاني وترد إلى زوجها الأول بعد الاستبراء بثلث حيض، وإن ولدت منه أولاد. وأما امرأة المفقود التي طلقت ولم تعلم بالرجعة، فإنه قد كان مالك يقول مرة: إذا تزوجتا ولم يدخل بهما أزواجهما فلا سبيل لأزواجهما إليهما. ثم إن مالكا وقت قبل موته بعام ونحوها في امرأة المطلق إذا أتت زوجها الأول ولم يدخل بها زوجها الآخر فقال مالك: زوجها الأول أحق بها. قال: وسمعت منه في المفقود أنه قال: هو أحق بها ما لم يدخل بها الثاني. وأنا أرى فيهما جميعا أن أزواجهما إذا أدركوهما قبل أن يدخل بهما أزواجهما هؤلاء الآخرون فالأولون أحق، وإن دخلوا فالآخرون. وقال أشهب مثل قوله واختار ما اختاره. وقال المغيرة وغيره: يقول مالك: الأول أحق. وقالوا: إن تمت امرأة زوجين توارث زوجاتهم ترجع إلى زوج غيرهم. وقال: ولم يستحل الفرج بعد الإعذار من السلطان بمنزلة عقد النكاح. وقد جاء زوجها ولم يطلق ولم يمت. قلت: أرأيت إن قدم زوجها بعد الأربع سنين وبعد الأربعة أشهر وعشر، ترد إليه في قول مالك ويكون أحق بها؟ قال: نظير. قلت: أفتكون عنده على تطليقتين؟ قال: لا ولكنها عنده على ثلاث تطليقات عند مالك، وإنما تكون على تطليقتين إذا رجعت إليه بعد زوج. قلت: أرأيت المفقود إذا أمر السلطان لامرأته أربع سنين ثم أعدت أربعة أشهر وعشرا، أيكون هذا الإعتداد تطليقة أم لا؟ قال: إن تزوجت ودخل بها فهي تطليقة. قلت: فإذا تزوجت بعد الأربعة الأشهر والعشر، الهجاء موته مات بعد الأربعة الأشهر والعشر، أقرته امرؤ؟ قال: إن أنكحت إن ماتت بعد نكاح ما قبل دخوله بها ورثت زوجها الأول، وإن مات فهو أحق بها، فهو كمجيئه. إن لو جاء وعلم أنه حي فريض بينه وبين الآخر. داعتدت من الأول من يوم مات لأن عقدة الأول لم تسقط وإنها تسقط بدخول الآخر بها. ولذلك إذا مات الزوج الآخر قبل دخوله بها فورثته، ثم انكشف أن الزوج الأول مات بعده أو قبله وبعد نكاحه أو جاءات الزوج الأول حي بطل ميراثها من الزوج الآخر وردت إلى الأول إن كان حيا وأخذت ميراثه إن كان ميتا. قلت: أرأيت امرأة المفقود تعتد أربع سنين في قول مالك بغير أمر السلطان؟ قال: قال مالك: لا، وإن أقامت عشرين سنة ثم رفعت أمرها إلى السلطان نظر فيها وكتب إلى موضعه الذي خرج إليه، فإن بأس منه ضرب لها من تلك الساعة أربع سنين. فقيل لمالك: هل تعتد بعد الأربع سنين عدة الوفاة أربعة أشهر وعشر من عيران بأمرها السلطان بذلك؟ قال: نعم. قيل: فلها دوما السلطان في الأربعة أشهر وعشرا بعد الأربع سنين؟ قال: لا. لا هي منددة. وقال مالك: يوقف مال المفقود والسلطان ينظر في ذلك، ولا يوقف اله ولا يداع أحد فيه ولا يبذره. وقال رب بعدين أبي عبد الرحمن: المفقود الذي لا بيان له سلطان ولا كتاب السلطان قداخل اهله وإمامه.
This is regarding the missing [husband] in Al-Mudawwana, in the chapter of the missing. I said: Have you seen a woman whose husband is reported dead, so she observes iddah from him, then marries; and a woman whose husband divorces her in such a way that she learns of the divorce and he takes her back during the iddah, but her husband (the first) has disappeared and she does not know of the return until the iddah ends, so she marries; and the wife of a missing man who observes iddah for four years by order of the sultan, then four months and ten days, then marries. Are these all treated the same according to Malik? He said: No. As for the one whose husband is reported dead, she is separated from her second husband and returned to her first husband after istibra' with three menstrual cycles, even if she had children from him. As for the wife of the missing man who was divorced and did not know of the return, Malik used to say once: If they married and their husbands did not consummate with them, then their husbands have no way to them. Then shortly before his death, regarding a divorced woman who goes to her first husband and the other husband has not consummated, Malik said: Her first husband is more entitled to her. He said: And I heard from him regarding the missing [husband] that he said: He is more entitled to her as long as the second has not consummated. And I see in both cases that if their husbands catch up with them before their later husbands consummate, then the first husbands are more entitled; but if they have consummated, then the later husbands [are more entitled]. And Ashhab said the same and chose what he chose. And Al-Mughirah and others said: Malik said the first is more entitled. And they said: If a woman has two husbands and inherits from them, she returns to none of them. And he said: The private part is not made permissible after authorization from the sultan except by a marriage contract. And her husband came and did not divorce nor die. I said: Have you seen if her husband returns after the four years and after the four months and ten days, is she returned to him according to Malik and is he more entitled to her? He said: Equivalent. I said: Is she considered with him on two divorces? He said: No, but with him she is on three divorces according to Malik, and she is only on two divorces if she returns to him after a husband. I said: Have you seen the missing husband if the sultan orders his wife [to wait] four years, then she observes four months and ten days, is this waiting considered a divorce or not? He said: If she marries and he consummates, then it is a divorce. I said: If she marries after the four months and ten days, and the missing one dies after the four months and ten days, did he attest it? He said: If she remarries, if she dies after the marriage before his consummation, she inherits from her first husband, and if he (the first) dies, he is more entitled to her, so it is like his coming. If he came and it was known he was alive, then a dispute between him and the other. She observes iddah from the first from the day he died because the tie of the first is not broken, and it is broken by the consummation of the other. Therefore, if the other husband dies before consummation, she inherits from him; then if it is discovered that the first husband died after or before his marriage, or if the first husband came alive, her inheritance from the other husband is void, and she is returned to the first if he is alive, and she takes his inheritance if he is dead. I said: Have you seen the wife of a missing person observing iddah for four years according to Malik without the sultan's order? He said: Malik said: No; even if she waited twenty years, then raised her matter to the sultan, he would look into it and write to the place he went; if there is news of him, he sets for her from that hour four years. So it was said to Malik: Does she observe after the four years an iddah of death (four months and ten days) from the beginning with the sultan's order? He said: Yes. It was said: So she has continuity (?) with the sultan's order for the four months and ten days after the four years? He said: No. She is not one of those. And Malik said: The wealth of the missing person is kept, and the sultan looks into that; it is not kept in a way that prevents ... nor is it wasted. And Rabi'ah ibn Abi Abd al-Rahman said: The missing person who has no statement from the sultan nor a letter from the sultan, his family and imam take charge.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
Passage type: This is a juridical discussion (mas'alah) regarding the rulings of a missing husband (mafqud) from the Maliki school, presented as a dialogue between Sahnun and Ibn al-Qasim (or similar) from Al-Mudawwana. The author examines three scenarios: (1) a woman whose husband is reported dead, (2) a woman whose husband divorces her, takes her back, but then disappears, and (3) a wife of a missing husband who waits four years by sultan's order, then four months and ten days. The core issue is whether upon the return of the first husband, the second marriage is nullified. According to Malik, in the first case, the second marriage is dissolved and she returns to her first husband after istibra' (a waiting period of three clean periods). In the second case, there is a difference in Malik's own statements: initially he said if the second marriage was not consummated, the first husband has no claim; later he said the first husband is more entitled if no consummation. For the missing husband case, Malik rules that the first husband is more entitled if the second has not consummated; if consummated, the second remains. This shows the Maliki position that consummation establishes the second marriage. The passage also discusses whether the waiting period constitutes a divorce (it does only if she remarries and consummates), inheritance issues, and the necessity of sultan's permission for the iddah of the missing. As a Hanafi text, the author likely presents this to contrast with the Hanafi position or to derive a stratagem (hila) for similar situations. Hanafi fiqh differs: in the Hanafi school, the wife of a missing husband waits for his age limit (usually 90 years) or until evidence of death, and she cannot remarry without confirmation. The passage serves as a comparative fiqh exercise for understanding how different schools handle similar dilemmas.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A husband who has disappeared and whose status (life or death) is unknown.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during her iddah in a revocable divorce.
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): A waiting period to ensure the womb is free from pregnancy before returning to previous husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki position presented in this passage, if a missing husband returns after his wife has remarried and the second husband has consummated the marriage, what happens to the first marriage?
A) The first marriage is restored immediately.
B) The wife is given the choice between the two husbands.
C) The second marriage remains valid and the first husband has no claim.
D) Both marriages are dissolved.

Key Takeaway
In the Maliki school, the second marriage of a missing husband's wife is valid if consummated, overriding the first husband's claim.

Inheritance and the Binding of the Marriage Tie in Maliki Law
Maliki inheritance discussion for mafqud wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the inheritance consequences and the effect of consummation on marriage ties for the missing husband's wife according to the Maliki school, and contrast it with Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Consummation breaks the first marriage tie, affecting inheritance and the widow's right to return.

This portion of the passage delves into the legal intricacies of inheritance and the continuity of the marriage tie (uqdah) when a missing husband's wife remarries. The Maliki school holds that only consummation of the second marriage severs the first marriage tie, leading to unique inheritance rules. Understanding this is essential for a Hanafi mufti to correctly navigate cross-madhhab inheritance questions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key concepts: 'uqdah' and 'consummation'
The passage uses the term 'عقدة الأول' (tie of the first) and states it is broken by 'دخول الآخر' (consummation of the other). Understand that marriage tie is a legal bond that affects inheritance and the right to return.
2
Extract the inheritance rules from the passage
If the second husband dies before consummation, she inherits from him; but if later it is discovered that the first husband died after the marriage, her inheritance from the second is void and she inherits from the first. The passage gives several permutations.
Focus on the phrase: 'وإن مات فهو أحق بها' and the subsequent discussion.
3
Trace the logic: the tie is broken only by consummation
The first marriage tie remains intact until the second consummates. That is why if the second husband dies before consummation, the tie with the first is still intact, affecting inheritance and the right to return.
This is a distinctive Maliki principle not shared by Hanafi.
4
Contrast with Hanafi inheritance for missing husband cases
In Hanafi, the wife cannot remarry while the first husband is missing; thus, no second marriage to complicate inheritance. If she remarries improperly, the second marriage is invalid, so inheritance does not transfer to the second husband. Understanding the difference helps in estate planning across madhhabs.

ترد إليه في قول مالك ويكون أحق بها؟ قال: نظير. قلت: أفتكون عنده على تطليقتين؟ قال: لا ولكنها عنده على ثلاث تطليقات عند مالك، وإنما تكون على تطليقتين إذا رجعت إليه بعد زوج. قلت: أرأيت المفقود إذا أمر والعشر، أقرته امرؤ؟ قال: إن أنكحت إن ماتت بعد نكاح ما قبل دخوله بها ورثت زوجها الأول، وإن مات فهو أحق بها، فهو كمجيئه. إن لو جاء وعلم أنه حي فريض بينه وبين الآخر. داعتدت من الأول من يوم مات لأن عقدة الأول لم تسقط وإنها تسقط بدخول الآخر بها. ولذلك إذا مات الزوج الآخر قبل دخوله بها فورثته، ثم انكشف أن الزوج الأول مات بعده أو قبله وبعد نكاحه أو جاءات الزوج الأول حي بطل ميراثها من الزوج الآخر وردت إلى الأول إن كان حيا وأخذت ميراثه إن كان ميتا. قلت: أرأيت امرأة المفقود تعتد أربع سنين في قول مالك بغير أمر السلطان؟ قال: قال مالك: لا، وإن أقامت عشرين سنة ثم رفعت أمرها إلى السلطان نظر فيها وكتب إلى موضعه الذي خرج إليه، فإن بأس منه ضرب لها من تلك الساعة أربع سنين. فقيل لمالك: هل تعتد بعد الأربع سنين عدة الوفاة أربعة أشهر وعشر من عيران بأمرها السلطان بذلك؟ قال: نعم. قيل: فلها دوما السلطان في الأربعة أشهر وعشرا بعد الأربع سنين؟ قال: لا. لا هي منددة. وقال مالك: يوقف مال المفقود والسلطان ينظر في ذلك، ولا يوقف اله ولا يداع أحد فيه ولا يبذره. وقال رب بعدين أبي عبد الرحمن: المفقود الذي لا بيان له سلطان ولا كتاب السلطان قداخل اهله وإمامه.
This is regarding the missing [husband] in Al-Mudawwana, in the chapter of the missing. I said: Have you seen a woman whose husband is reported dead, so she observes iddah from him, then marries; and a woman whose husband divorces her in such a way that she learns of the divorce and he takes her back during the iddah, but her husband (the first) has disappeared and she does not know of the return until the iddah ends, so she marries; and the wife of a missing man who observes iddah for four years by order of the sultan, then four months and ten days, then marries. Are these all treated the same according to Malik? He said: No. As for the one whose husband is reported dead, she is separated from her second husband and returned to her first husband after istibra' with three menstrual cycles, even if she had children from him. As for the wife of the missing man who was divorced and did not know of the return, Malik used to say once: If they married and their husbands did not consummate with them, then their husbands have no way to them. Then shortly before his death, regarding a divorced woman who goes to her first husband and the other husband has not consummated, Malik said: Her first husband is more entitled to her. He said: And I heard from him regarding the missing [husband] that he said: He is more entitled to her as long as the second has not consummated. And I see in both cases that if their husbands catch up with them before their later husbands consummate, then the first husbands are more entitled; but if they have consummated, then the later husbands [are more entitled]. And Ashhab said the same and chose what he chose. And Al-Mughirah and others said: Malik said the first is more entitled. And they said: If a woman has two husbands and inherits from them, she returns to none of them. And he said: The private part is not made permissible after authorization from the sultan except by a marriage contract. And her husband came and did not divorce nor die. I said: Have you seen if her husband returns after the four years and after the four months and ten days, is she returned to him according to Malik and is he more entitled to her? He said: Equivalent. I said: Is she considered with him on two divorces? He said: No, but with him she is on three divorces according to Malik, and she is only on two divorces if she returns to him after a husband. I said: Have you seen the missing husband if the sultan orders his wife [to wait] four years, then she observes four months and ten days, is this waiting considered a divorce or not? He said: If she marries and he consummates, then it is a divorce. I said: If she marries after the four months and ten days, and the missing one dies after the four months and ten days, did he attest it? He said: If she remarries, if she dies after the marriage before his consummation, she inherits from her first husband, and if he (the first) dies, he is more entitled to her, so it is like his coming. If he came and it was known he was alive, then a dispute between him and the other. She observes iddah from the first from the day he died because the tie of the first is not broken, and it is broken by the consummation of the other. Therefore, if the other husband dies before consummation, she inherits from him; then if it is discovered that the first husband died after or before his marriage, or if the first husband came alive, her inheritance from the other husband is void, and she is returned to the first if he is alive, and she takes his inheritance if he is dead. I said: Have you seen the wife of a missing person observing iddah for four years according to Malik without the sultan's order? He said: Malik said: No; even if she waited twenty years, then raised her matter to the sultan, he would look into it and write to the place he went; if there is news of him, he sets for her from that hour four years. So it was said to Malik: Does she observe after the four years an iddah of death (four months and ten days) from the beginning with the sultan's order? He said: Yes. It was said: So she has continuity (?) with the sultan's order for the four months and ten days after the four years? He said: No. She is not one of those. And Malik said: The wealth of the missing person is kept, and the sultan looks into that; it is not kept in a way that prevents ... nor is it wasted. And Rabi'ah ibn Abi Abd al-Rahman said: The missing person who has no statement from the sultan nor a letter from the sultan, his family and imam take charge.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
Passage type: This is a juridical discussion (mas'alah) regarding the rulings of a missing husband (mafqud) from the Maliki school, presented as a dialogue between Sahnun and Ibn al-Qasim (or similar) from Al-Mudawwana. The author examines three scenarios: (1) a woman whose husband is reported dead, (2) a woman whose husband divorces her, takes her back, but then disappears, and (3) a wife of a missing husband who waits four years by sultan's order, then four months and ten days. The core issue is whether upon the return of the first husband, the second marriage is nullified. According to Malik, in the first case, the second marriage is dissolved and she returns to her first husband after istibra' (a waiting period of three clean periods). In the second case, there is a difference in Malik's own statements: initially he said if the second marriage was not consummated, the first husband has no claim; later he said the first husband is more entitled if no consummation. For the missing husband case, Malik rules that the first husband is more entitled if the second has not consummated; if consummated, the second remains. This shows the Maliki position that consummation establishes the second marriage. The passage also discusses whether the waiting period constitutes a divorce (it does only if she remarries and consummates), inheritance issues, and the necessity of sultan's permission for the iddah of the missing. As a Hanafi text, the author likely presents this to contrast with the Hanafi position or to derive a stratagem (hila) for similar situations. Hanafi fiqh differs: in the Hanafi school, the wife of a missing husband waits for his age limit (usually 90 years) or until evidence of death, and she cannot remarry without confirmation. The passage serves as a comparative fiqh exercise for understanding how different schools handle similar dilemmas.

Key Terms
عقدة (uqdah): The marriage tie or bond; a legal relationship that entails rights and obligations.
دخول (dukhul): Consummation of marriage; sexual intercourse between spouses.
إرث (irth): Inheritance; the transfer of property from a deceased person to his/her legal heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki school, what event severs the marriage tie of the first husband?
A) The wife's remarriage with sultan's permission.
B) The passage of four years.
C) The consummation of the second marriage.
D) The death of the second husband.

Key Takeaway
In the Maliki school, only consummation of the second marriage breaks the first husband's marriage tie, affecting inheritance and the right to return.

Procedure for Wife of Absent Husband to Obtain Divorce (Maliki Hila)
Maliki procedure for wife of absent husband to obtain divorce due to lack of nafaqah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the step-by-step Maliki procedure for a wife of an absent husband to obtain a revocable divorce due to lack of nafaqah, and identify the conditions and oaths required.

Core Idea
An absent husband's wife can obtain a revocable divorce after proving absence, a one-month wait, and an oath.

This lesson presents a legal stratagem (hila) from the Maliki school that allows a wife to free herself from a marriage where her husband is absent and not providing maintenance. The procedure is detailed in classical Maliki sources and offers a practical solution for a dire situation. Understanding this procedure provides insight into how different madhhabs address marital hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's claim and the judge's role
The wife claims no maintenance from her absent husband. She approaches the judge (or the Muslim community) and seeks divorce. The judge requires her to prove the marriage and the husband's absence.
The judge acts as the authority to verify the situation and set the procedure.
2
List the evidence required
The wife must prove the marriage (marriage contract) and the husband's absence. Witnesses must testify that they know of no provision left for the wife by the husband, and that he did not appoint anyone to provide for her.
The testimony is 'on her statement' – the witnesses affirm that they have no knowledge of provision.
3
Explain the one-month waiting period and the wife's oath
After evidence, the judge sets a term of one month. If the husband does not appear or provide within that month, the wife is to take an oath similar to the witnesses' testimony (swearing that she has no maintenance). Then she is permitted to pronounce a revocable divorce on herself.
The waiting period ensures that the husband is truly absent and cannot be reached.
4
Describe the outcome: revocable divorce and husband's options
The divorce is revocable (talāq rajʿī). If the husband dies during the ʿiddah, he can take her back (the divorce is voided, and she inherits). If he returns but is unable to pay, she may choose to stay without maintenance, and if she is free and agrees to spend from her own wealth, she may do so without her guardian's interference.
This preserves the marriage bond if possible, which is better than divorce.

Source Text

وأما المتعنت الممتنع عن الاتفاق ففى مجموع الامير بالنصر نفقة الحال نلها الضيام فان لم ثيبت عسرة الفق او طلق والا طلق عليه قال محشية قوله والاطلق عليه الحاكم من غير
The absence of her husband from the judge or the Muslim community due to her claim of no maintenance from his wealth, claiming that he has not left her what to spend, nor sent it to her, nor appointed anyone to spend on her, and she seeks divorce and swears on that, so the judge divorces him, or orders her to divorce herself, and he judges it. End. And in the book Jami' Umm Masai'il al-Ahkam fi Qat' al-Khisam, from what the judge is in dire need of, by Shaykh al-Dars ibn Khalid al-Maliki, its text: The sixth [section]: on the insolvency of the absent husband. When a wife comes to the judge, he requires her to prove the marriage and prove the absence, and if they (the witnesses) do not know that he left for her anything nor commissioned her, they testify to that upon her statement. Then he sets for her a term of one month. And in Tuhfat al-Hukkam: The wife of the absent husband, when she desires separation from her husband, is given a month. If the term expires and the man [does not return?], she swears an oath similar to what the witnesses testified to, and divorces herself with a revocable divorce. If he dies during her iddah, he has the right to take her back. And if he returns but is unable to pay, she has the option to stay without nafaqah, and if she is a free woman and agrees to stay without nafaqah provided she spends from her own wealth, that is permissible, and her guardian has no say, because if she divorces, she must spend on herself, so staying with the husband is better because it preserves the form [of marriage]. End. Ruling on the rebellious husband who neglects the rights. As for the recalcitrant who refuses to spend, in Majmu' al-Amir [??]: He is forced to pay current nafaqah or else he is imprisoned, and if he does not prove hardship, he is divorced, otherwise he is divorced upon him. The annotator said: His statement 'and the judge divorces him without [??]'
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a procedural mechanism (hila) for a wife whose husband is absent and not providing maintenance. The text begins with a summary: the wife can go to the judge or to the Muslim community, claim that the husband left no money, sent nothing, and appointed no agent. She takes an oath, and the judge either pronounces divorce or orders her to divorce herself. Then it quotes a more detailed procedure from a Maliki source, Jami' Umm Masai'il al-Ahkam. The judge requires the wife to prove the marriage and the husband's absence. Witnesses testify that they have no knowledge of the husband leaving any provision. Then the judge sets a one-month waiting period. If the husband does not appear or provide within that month, the wife is to take an oath similar to the witnesses' testimony and then pronounce a revocable divorce on herself. The text adds from Tuhfat al-Hukkam that if the husband dies during her iddah, he can take her back (meaning the divorce is revoked and she inherits). If the husband later returns but is unable to pay, she can choose to stay without maintenance; if she is free and agrees to stay and spend on herself, she may do so without her guardian's interference. This is because staying married preserves the marriage bond, which is better than divorce. The passage then shifts to the ruling on the recalcitrant (mutaanit) husband who refuses to pay while present. According to Majmu' al-Amir, he is forced to pay current nafaqah; if he fails to prove hardship, he is divorced by the judge. The annotator adds that the judge divorces him without [further condition?]. The text ends abruptly. This lesson focuses on the absent husband procedure as a legal stratagem to resolve the wife's predicament without forcing her into an undesired marriage.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-gha'ib): A husband who is absent from his wife's location and cannot be reached, and is not providing maintenance
المتعنت (al-muta'annit): A husband who is present but refuses to provide maintenance without a valid excuse
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): Revocable divorce; the husband can take his wife back during the iddah without a new contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She must immediately pronounce divorce herself.
D) She must wait for one year.

Key Takeaway
The Maliki procedure provides a structured, judge-supervised way for a wife of an absent husband to obtain a revocable divorce, preserving her rights while maintaining the possibility of reconciliation.

Hila for Wife of Absent Husband to Obtain Divorce
Explaining a hila for a wife to divorce when husband absents and withholds maintenance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions and procedure of the Hanafi hila for a wife to obtain a judicial divorce due to an absent husband's failure to provide maintenance, and apply it to a modern case.

Core Idea
A wife can divorce herself by swearing after a waiting period when her absent husband fails to provide maintenance.

This lesson examines a legal stratagem (hila) that allows a wife to obtain a divorce when her husband is absent and does not provide maintenance. The source text presents both Hanafi and Maliki approaches, showing how the wife can proceed through judicial process. Understanding this hila is essential for muftis dealing with modern cases of absent husbands and maintenance claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
The wife's husband is absent and does not provide maintenance. She needs a way to obtain a divorce without his presence.
Absence must be established.
2
Recognize the Conditions
The wife must prove marriage and the husband's absence, claim lack of maintenance, and swear an oath.
Witnesses may be needed.
3
Understand the Procedure
The judge sets a deadline (e.g., one month). If the husband does not return or send maintenance, the wife swears and divorces herself with a revocable divorce.
The judge may also order the husband to divorce.
4
Note the Effect
The divorce is revocable (talaq raj'i), allowing the husband to take her back during the iddah if he returns.
This preserves the possibility of reconciliation.
5
Consider Cross-Madhhab Variations
The Maliki text quoted has a similar procedure but emphasizes a one-month deadline and the wife's oath on witness testimony.
The Hanafi view does not require a fixed deadline.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) for a wife whose husband is absent and fails to provide maintenance. The passage first describes the Hanafi ruling: the wife complains to the judge or to Muslims, claims lack of maintenance, and swears. The judge then either orders the husband to divorce or allows the wife to divorce herself. Then it quotes a Maliki text detailing a similar procedure: the wife must prove marriage and absence, a deadline of one month is set, and if the husband does not appear or send maintenance, she swears and divorces herself with a revocable divorce. The Maliki text also discusses nuances about returning during iddah and the possibility of the wife agreeing to stay without maintenance. As a Hanafi work, the author likely presents this for comparison or to show the acceptability of such a stratagem. The key point is that the wife can obtain a judicial divorce through swearing.

Key Terms
غيبة الزوج (ghaybat al-zawj): Absence of the husband such that he is not present to provide maintenance or communicate.
حلفت (halafat): The wife swears an oath to support her claim of lack of maintenance.
طلقة رجعية (talaqa raj'iyya): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back the wife during the waiting period.

The Case of the Rebellious Husband (Mutamattin): A Legal Stratagem for Maintenance Refusal
Addressing the case of an obstinate husband who refuses maintenance and the judge's intervention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ruling on a husband who obstinately refuses maintenance and apply the Hanafi legal stratagem (hila) of judicial divorce.

Core Idea
A judge can divorce a husband who refuses maintenance, even without his consent, to relieve the wife's hardship.

This passage addresses the situation of a husband who is mutamattin—obstinate and heedless of his wife's right to maintenance. In Hanafi fiqh, maintenance (nafaqa) is a wajib obligation. When a husband with means refuses to provide it, the wife has legal recourse. The passage presents a legal stratagem (hila): the judge can pronounce divorce on the husband's behalf if he persists in refusal and cannot prove poverty. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the conditions, and the scholarly discussion around this intervention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
Recognize that the husband is mutamattin—he refuses maintenance without valid reason. This is the trigger for the hila.
Mutamattin is analogous to nushuz for a wife.
2
State the default ruling
Maintenance is wajib on the husband. If he fails to pay and cannot prove poverty, he must either pay or divorce; otherwise the judge may compel divorce.
The text from Majmu' al-Amir says: 'the present maintenance is obligatory? If he does not prove poverty, then he must divorce, otherwise the judge divorces him.'
3
Analyze the Maliki dispute
Ibn al-Qasim holds that the wife may separate because separation is necessary. Ibn Abd al-Rahman says she cannot, because the cause of separation (lack of maintenance) is agreed upon—implying only the judge can terminate the marriage.
Both views are within the Maliki school; the Hanafi position differs.
4
Extract the hila
The hila (legal stratagem) is that the judge divorces the husband on his behalf, relieving the wife without requiring her to initiate a lengthy process.
This stratagem is based on necessity (darura) and the principle of removing hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عورت کے حقوق سے لاپرواہ سرکش کا حکم وأما المتعنت الممتنع عن الاتفاق ففى مجموع الامير بالنصر نفقة الحال نلها الضيام فان لم ثيبت عسرة الفق او طلق والا طلق عليه قال محشية قوله والاطلق عليه الحاكم من غير تلوم إلى ان قال إن تطوع بالنفتة قريب نو اجنبي فقال ابن القاسملها ان تفارق لان الفراق مدوجب لما و الي ابن عبد الرحمن لا مقال لها لان سبب الفراق هو عدم النفقة قدا متفى وهو الذى تفضيه المدول كما قال ابن المناصف انظر الخطاب انتهى
Ruling on a rebellious husband heedless of wife's rights. As for the obstinate one who refuses to provide maintenance, in Majmu' al-Amir bil-Nasr: the present maintenance is obligatory? If he does not prove poverty, then he must divorce, otherwise the judge divorces him. The commentator said: the judge divorces him without delay? Until he said: if a relative or stranger volunteers to provide maintenance, then Ibn al-Qasim said she may separate because separation is obligatory... and Ibn Abd al-Rahman said she has no say because the cause of separation is lack of maintenance which is agreed upon... as Ibn al-Manasif said... look at al-Khattab. End.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage deals with a husband who is rebellious and refuses to provide maintenance (nafaqa). It first states that in the Majmu' al-Amir, the current maintenance is obligatory, and if the husband cannot prove his poverty, he must either pay or divorce; otherwise the judge divorces him on his behalf. The commentary then discusses a Maliki dispute: Ibn al-Qasim holds that the wife can separate because separation is necessary, while Ibn Abd al-Rahman holds she cannot, because the cause of separation (lack of maintenance) is agreed upon. The passage ends by referencing Ibn al-Manasif and al-Khattab. The hila here is that the judge can force a divorce to relieve the wife of hardship. The author presents this as part of legal stratagems to deal with a difficult husband.

Key Terms
متعنت (mutamattin): A husband who is obstinate and refuses to fulfill his obligation of maintenance without valid reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The judge divorces the husband on his behalf.
C) The wife can marry another man immediately.
D) The husband is imprisoned for life.

Key Takeaway
The judge has authority to divorce a husband who obdurately refuses maintenance, providing a vital hila to protect the wife's welfare.

When Does the Waiting Period for an Impotent Husband Begin?
Maliki ruling on the start of the waiting period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Maliki ruling on when the waiting period for an impotent husband begins and apply it to a wife's complaint scenario.

Core Idea
The waiting period for an impotent husband begins from the wife's complaint, not from marriage.

In cases of impotence, the wife has the right to seek separation. But when does the clock start? The source passage discusses a crucial detail: the period begins only when the wife formally complains to the judge. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its practical implications for a mufti advising a distressed wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The source begins by asking: 'When is the period imposed? From the day he married her or from the day she raises the matter to the sultan?' This is the key issue.
The answer shapes the entire timeline.
2
Extract the Maliki answer
The text states: 'He said: from the day she raises it to the sultan.' This is attributed to Malik. Note the condition: formal complaint to the judge triggers the period.
This protects the husband from a premature penalty.
3
Contrast with possible alternative
One might think the period starts automatically from the marriage date. The Maliki ruling explicitly rejects that, making the complaint the operative event.
This gives the husband time to seek treatment before any pressure.
4
Apply to a mufti scenario
Imagine a wife asks: 'My husband has been impotent for two years, but I never complained to a judge. Can I now seek separation?' According to the Maliki view, the one-year waiting period would start from her complaint, not from the marriage date.
The mufti must advise her to formally raise the matter to the court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما السوال عن حكم زوجة العنين نجوابها في المدته قال الوات العنين متى يضرب له الرجل من يوم تزوجها او من يوم ترفعه إلى السلطان قال من يوم ترفيه إلى السلطان وكذا و قال مالك قلت الايت العنين ادا فرق بينهما ايكون املك بها في العدة قال قال ملك لا يكون املك بها في العدة ولا رجعة له عليها قلت ارأيت الحنين انا لو يجامع امرأته في السنة فوق بينهما بعد السنته ا يكون لها نصف الصداق قال قال مالك لما الصداق كلد كاملا - قال مالك وبلغنى عن سليمان بن بساران ان قال اجل المعترض عن اهله ستة ابن القاسم عن مالك عن ابن شهاب عن ابن المسيب انه قال اذا دخل الرجل بامرأته فاعترض عنها فانه يضرب له اجل سته فان استطاع ان يمسها والا فرق بينهما ابن وهب قال موسى بن على وقال ابن شهاب ان القضاة يقضون في الذى لا يستطيع امرأته يتربس سنة يبتغى فيها لنفسه فان الم في ذلك باهله فهي امرأته وان مضت سنة ولم يمسها ما فرق بينه وبينها ويقضى القضاة بذلك من حين تناكرة امرأته ويناكره اهلها قال ابن شهاب وان كانت تحته امراته فولدت له ثم اعرض عنها فلم يستطع لها فلم اسمع احدا فرق بين رجل ومين امرأته بعد ان يمسها وهذا الامر عندنا قلت ارأيت العنين ايجوز له
As for the question about the ruling of the wife of an impotent man, the answer during her waiting period: The impotent man, when is the period imposed? From the day he married her or from the day she raises the matter to the sultan? He said: from the day she raises it to the sultan. Likewise Malik said: I said: when they are separated, does he have the right to take her back during the iddah? He said: Malik said he does not have the right to take her back during the iddah, nor does he have the right of return. I said: do you think that if the impotent man does not have intercourse with his wife for a year, then they are separated after the year, does she get half the dowry? He said: Malik said the full dowry is obligatory. Malik said: it reached me from Sulayman ibn Yasar that the period for one who refrains from his wife is six months. Ibn al-Qasim from Malik from Ibn Shihab from Ibn al-Musayyab: when a man consummates with his wife then refrains from her, a six-month period is imposed. If he can have intercourse, otherwise they are separated. Ibn Wahab said Musa ibn Ali said Ibn Shihab said: judges rule regarding one who cannot have intercourse with his wife: he waits for a year to seek treatment. If he succeeds, she is his wife. If a year passes without him touching her, they are separated. The judges rule that from the time the wife complains. Ibn Shihab said: if a man's wife gave birth to him then he refrained from her... I have not heard that anyone separated a man from his wife after he had consummated... this is our ruling. I said: do you think the impotent man is permissible for him...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of a husband who is impotent ('anin). It records opinions from the Maliki school, primarily attributed to Imam Malik and his disciples. The key question is when the period of waiting (ajal) begins: from the marriage date or from the wife's complaint. The answer is from the complaint. Then the period is set: some say one year, others six months. During that period, if the husband can consummate, the marriage continues; otherwise the judge separates them. However, once consummation has occurred (i.e., the husband has had intercourse at least once), there is no separation due to impotence. The author includes these rulings to show how the issue is handled in the Maliki school, likely for comparison with Hanafi or as part of a broader discussion of legal stratagems for marital problems.

Key Terms
العنين (al-'anin): A man who is impotent and unable to have intercourse with his wife.
أجل (ajal): A waiting period or term fixed by law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The waiting period for an impotent husband begins from the wife's complaint to the judge, not from the marriage date.
